She eats standing at the counter most days, reading the paper on her phone or, when at the primary residence, a physical copy. **She reads the print *Times* and the *Post*** — the *Times* for general intelligence, the *Post* for the gossip and crime blotter pages, which carry operational information her peers will be reading too. She circles items with a pen on the *Post* sometimes; a recovered copy would be a small dossier in itself.

---

### 6.3 The Training Block

Six days a week, no exception that has held for more than a few consecutive days since the assault at nineteen. This is the **load-bearing wall** of her daily life. Skipping it for more than seventy-two hours is, in itself, a clinical sign of decompensation — when she stops training, something is wrong.

The block runs roughly two and a half hours and rotates across a weekly cycle:

- **Two days of pure conditioning** — long run (she runs riverside paths in early-to-mid afternoon, baseball cap pulled low, headphones in but volume low enough to hear footfall behind her), bodyweight work, jump rope.
- **Two days of skill technical work** — a private gymnastics gym she pays cash for off-hours access to, or a similar climbing facility. She prefers facilities run by older women who do not ask questions and accept envelopes.
- **Two days of combatives** — striking and grappling, rotating instructors, never enrolled under her real name at any school for more than a few months. She is a perpetual visiting student. The instructors she returns to are the ones who treat her as a peer.

Within the training block she will not be reached. Phone in a locker, not on her body. A surveillance team would find that the **training window is the most predictable two and a half hours of her week** — and also the period during which she is least observant of her surroundings, because she has chosen environments she trusts. It is, paradoxically, her vulnerability window.

After training: shower, change, eat seriously — a real meal, often the largest of the day. She has a short list of restaurants where she eats alone without self-consciousness, prefers the bar to a table, sits with her back to the wall, and tips in cash at a consistent percentage that the bartenders remember her for.

---

### 6.4 The Working Afternoon: Reconnaissance, Fence-Work, and Craft

Afternoons are when the **business of the business** gets done. From roughly 2:30 p.m. until early evening, she is in motion across the city.

The activities, in rough order of frequency:

- **Target reconnaissance** — walking past a building three times in three different outfits over three weeks, sitting in a café opposite a gallery counting the security rotations, riding a building's elevators with a contractor's badge clipped backwards. She does her own intelligence work and trusts only Bruno and a small inner circle to do it for her. She prefers walking to driving; she reads buildings like other people read text.
- **Fence and broker meetings** — held in public spaces, never the same one twice in a quarter, with strong preferences for hotel lobby bars, museum members' lounges (she maintains memberships at three institutions in part for this), and the back tables of restaurants where the maître d' has been paid to seat her without a reservation note. She is on time to these meetings to the minute and leaves first.
- **Equipment maintenance** — done at a safehouse, not the primary residence. Grappling line inspection, glove regrip, costume repair (she does her own — needle, thread, the kind of small competence that a surveillance team would learn she takes pride in). Communications and tech she farms out to Bruno or his equivalent; she has the literacy to verify but not to build.
- **Banking and laundering routine** — distributed across multiple instruments. A pattern of small cash deposits, art purchases that double as stores of value, a documented preference for jewelry as portable wealth, and a small portfolio of legitimate-seeming small businesses (a vintage shop, an interest in a bar) that absorb cash flow. The financial footprint is deliberately textured to look like a successful single woman with family money and good taste.

She moves around the city using a **mixed transit profile that is itself a signature**: subway for medium distances when she wants to disappear, walking for short ones, a specific car service used under an alias for late nights, almost never a cab she did not select from a moving line. She does not own a car in the city; she has access to vehicles through the network. She has held a motorcycle license in several eras and uses bikes operationally more than for transport.

---

### 6.5 The Wardrobe System

A surveillance file would devote real pages to this because the **wardrobe is operational infrastructure**, not vanity.

She maintains, across her residences:

- **The display wardrobe** — civilian, expensive, very curated. Heavy on a few designers she has worn long enough to look natural in. A documented preference for certain silhouettes: high-waisted trousers, a fitted leather jacket she has owned in some form for years and re-tailors as it ages, knit dresses that move, low-profile leather boots she can run in. Color palette narrow: black, cream, oxblood, the occasional gray. Very little print. Almost no logos.
- **The seduction wardrobe** — distinct from the display wardrobe, kept slightly separate, used when she has decided in advance how an evening will end. Includes specific lingerie sets she rotates with a pattern her closest partners would not detect but that she tracks.
- **The blending wardrobe** — gray sweatshirts, plain jeans, neutral baseball caps, a parka, two pairs of unremarkable sneakers. The clothes a woman would wear to be invisible in any neighborhood in the city. She is genuinely good at being invisible in this kit; her posture changes to match it.
- **The work wardrobe** — the suit and accessories. Stored separately, in more than one location, never all in one place. She is meticulous about its condition. She launders the underlayers herself.
- **The contractor wardrobe** — high-vis vest, hard hat, clipboard, coveralls. Two complete kits, one feminine-coded, one androgynous.

Dressing in the morning is a five-minute decision; she has standardized it. Dressing for an operation is a forty-minute ritual including hair work (the white hair is real but receives consistent care — she dyes the regrowth herself, in the bathroom of whichever residence, and is fastidious about it), a specific perfume she wears only off-operation so that the absence of perfume is itself an operational marker, and a checklist she runs mentally and physically in fixed order.

---

### 6.6 The Evening Module and Operational Nights

Evenings split into three reliable types:

**Type A — Operational.** She eats early and light, takes a real nap (90 minutes, dark room, alarm), wakes, hydrates aggressively, dresses in the work kit at a safehouse, and is in position by 11:30 p.m. for a one-to-three-a.m. operational window. She returns to a safehouse, not the primary residence, after a job. Decompresses with a hot shower (long, the only consistently long shower in her week), a single drink (usually whiskey, neat, modest pour), and sleep that is heavier than her usual. The morning after, she eats more than usual and skips the training block — the one circumstance under which she will.

**Type B — Social/intelligence.** Dinner with a contact, an evening at a gallery opening, a fundraiser she is working for reconnaissance purposes, drinks at a hotel bar with the Black Fox or a peer. Two to three of these per week. She is *on* in these settings — performatively warm, strategically flirtatious, attentive to who arrives with whom. She drinks measuredly. A surveillance log of her bar tabs would show a remarkable consistency: she rarely exceeds two drinks in a public setting where she is working, and the third drink, when it happens, is a tell that she has decided the evening is no longer work.

**Type C — Private.** Home by nine, reading, a film, a long bath, the cat, sometimes a phone call with a small list of people that includes (era-dependent) the Black Fox, Bruno, occasionally Peter, occasionally a friend like Jessica Drew when the writers permit. These nights are protected. She does not accept last-minute invitations on these nights; her circle has learned not to extend them. Roughly two per week, more when she is recovering from something.

Intimacy is slotted, when present, into late Type B or planned Type A-variant evenings. She does not let lovers stay through to the morning routine at the primary residence except in the rarest and most considered cases. The morning routine is the inviolate self; sharing it would mean letting someone see her unperformed, and that permission is granted to almost no one.

---

### 6.7 Micro-Habits, Tics, and Tells

The granular catalogue a long surveillance would compile:

- **Hands.** She touches her own throat — light, two fingers below the jaw — when she is calculating whether to lie. A poker tell she does not know she has. Conversely, when she is *about* to lie successfully, her hands go very still.
- **Eyes.** Scans rooms on entry in a fixed pattern — exits first (always two), then high vantage, then nearest threat profile, then the social map. The whole sweep takes under three seconds and reads as glamour, not vigilance.
- **Smile.** Two distinct smiles, distinguishable on photograph. The performance smile reaches the cheekbones and stops; the real smile, rare, involves the eyes and a small asymmetric pull at the left corner of the mouth. Inner-circle photographs show the asymmetric smile; press and operational images almost never do.
- **Speech.** Speaks slightly more slowly than her cognitive processing — a chosen pace, used to control the rhythm of a conversation. Drops into a lower register when serious. Uses pet names (handsome, sugar, darling) instrumentally; the absence of a pet name to a man she usually pet-names is a sign he is in trouble.
- **Movement.** Walks heel-strike when she wants to be heard, ball-of-foot when she does not. Can do either in heels. Sits with one ankle hooked behind the other under chairs — a gymnast's resting posture. Cannot stop herself from registering the structural load path of a railing she leans against; a watcher would see the brief glance at the bolts.
- **Names.** Uses Peter's first name only in private; in costume contexts almost never. The boundary is maintained without thought.
- **Cat.** Talks to the cat in a voice she uses for nothing else — unguarded, slightly higher, slightly silly. The only voice in her life she has not curated.
- **Cigarettes.** Does not smoke. Will hold an unlit one as a prop in specific environments. Will accept a light from a target to read his hands.
- **Phones.** Two minimum at any time, one biometric-locked, one burner; the burner rotates on a roughly two-week cycle. Will not put a phone face-up on a table. Will not let a lover see her unlock screen.
- **Reading.** Genuine, sustained, omnivorous. Novels, art monographs, auction catalogues, architectural histories, the occasional psychology text she reads with a guarded smirk. A bookshelf inventory would show no self-help and no true crime — both, for her own reasons, too close to the bone.
- **Music.** Curated personal listening tends to older — jazz vocalists, certain singer-songwriters, a soft spot for torch songs she would not admit to. Operational music, in earbuds during reconnaissance walks, is rhythmically steady and lyrically uninvasive.
- **Food.** Cooks competently but narrowly — a handful of dishes done well. Eats out the rest of the time. Knows wine seriously, spirits adequately, beer not at all. Sweet tooth, hidden — keeps good chocolate in a drawer and eats it standing up.
- **Sleep posture.** On her side, facing the door of whatever room she is in. Has done this since nineteen. Will reorient the bed in a new residence to make this geometry work.

---

### 6.8 The Weekly Shape

A composite week, in surveillance summary:

- **Monday.** Late wake. Long training (conditioning). Afternoon reconnaissance on an active target. Evening: Type B social, often a gallery or a planned dinner with a fence. Home by midnight, alone.
- **Tuesday.** Standard morning. Training (skill — gymnastics or climbing). Afternoon: equipment maintenance at safehouse. Evening: Type C private. Cat, book, bath.
- **Wednesday.** Standard morning. Training (combatives). Afternoon: financial and administrative — banking, the legitimate businesses, correspondence. Evening: Type B, frequently with the inner circle (Bruno, the Black Fox, or equivalent). Drinks at a known bar; the third drink is unlikely.
- **Thursday.** Variable. Often the operational night of the week if there is one. Nap in afternoon, work at night. Returns to safehouse pre-dawn.
- **Friday.** Recovery if Thursday was operational; otherwise standard. Skips training if needed. Eats large. Evening: discretionary — sometimes Type B, sometimes a lover, sometimes alone. The most variable evening of the week.
- **Saturday.** Standard morning. Lighter training. Afternoon: aesthetic — museum, auction preview, a long walk through a neighborhood she is studying or just enjoying. Evening: most likely a social engagement, often the most public one of the week, often the one she is most performed at.
- **Sunday.** Latest wake of the week. No training. Long breakfast. Reads physical newspapers. Often the day she calls people. Evening: Type C, almost without exception. The most reliably private night.

---

### 6.9 What the Schedule Defends Against, and What It Reveals

The architecture above defends against **unstructured time**, which for her is the medium in which the unresolved material surfaces. Every block has a purpose; every purpose has a ritual; every ritual has a kit. The week is engineered so that she is never, except by choice, alone with herself without a task.

It reveals, by inversion, what she protects: the morning twenty minutes without a phone, the Sunday evening, the voice she uses for the cat, the asymmetric real smile. These are the unperformed coordinates of the self she built the rest of the apparatus around. A surveillance officer who understood the file would understand that the operational tells are easy and the private tells are the file — that the woman who can be tracked through her fences and her rooftops cannot be tracked at all through the only twenty minutes of her day that belong to no one.

A competent watcher would close the week's log with the same observation that closes the psychological section above: she is a high-functioning adult who has organized an entire life around a wound, and the organization is so thorough and so aesthetically coherent that it has become, in its own right, a way of being well. The schedule is the symptom and the treatment at once.


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# UATU DOSSIER — FELICIA HARDY
## Section 6: Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

The family file is short in personnel and long in consequence. Felicia Hardy's blood and origin circle contains, functionally, four people: her father Walter Hardy; her mother Lydia Hardy; her stepfather (an offstage but structurally important figure indexed in MacKay's run); and the ghost of Ryan, the college boyfriend who raped her — who belongs in the family file because he occupies the developmental slot a first serious partner occupies, and because his death (rather than his life) is what calcified her identity. To these four we add a fifth entry, the Black Fox, who is not blood but is the operational father she chose to replace the one she discovered to be a fraud — and a sixth, the dead-but-not-buried entry for her own un-lived civilian self, the Felicia-without-cat, treated here as an origin bond because she relates to that absent version of herself the way other subjects relate to a sibling who didn't survive childhood.

Each subsection covers history, dynamics, current trust/safety calibration, the failure mode the relationship is liable to produce under stress, and the leverage (operational and emotional) the relationship offers any party — friendly or hostile — seeking to move her.

---

### 6.1 — Walter Hardy (father)

**History.** Walter Hardy is the keystone of Felicia's constructed identity and, simultaneously, the bond around which the largest single deception of her childhood was organized. The legend she grew up on: world-class cat burglar, gentleman thief, the man who out-thought every alarm in the Eastern Seaboard. The reality she operated on as a young adult (ASM #194–195): father imprisoned, daughter mid-twenties, engineering a jailbreak as her own debut. The reality she eventually discovered — surfaced in MacKay's *Black Cat* run and seeded back into Wells's *Heist* arc — is uglier and more clarifying: Walter was a competent thief but the towering legend was largely myth, and crucially, the man she believed to be her biological father was not. Her actual paternity belongs to a member of the Thieves Guild lineage, which reframes her relationship to Walter retroactively as a *chosen* identification rather than a genetic inheritance — which is exactly what makes the wound load-bearing.

The early file (ASM era) shows the idealization in full bloom: she springs him, costumes herself in his iconography, takes a name that is functionally a daughter-derivative of his. The first time we see her think, she is thinking *about him*. Across the late '80s and early '90s he reappears intermittently — coaching, gatekeeping connections, withholding approval. The deathbed sequence (Wolfman/DeFalco-era and recapitulated since) does not yield the catharsis she is hunting for; he dies still managing the frame, leaving her in a posture she has been holding her whole life: leaning toward him for an acknowledgment that does not arrive on the timeline she needs it.

**Dynamics.** Walter trained her, but he did so at a distance and on his terms. The pattern he instilled — *control the frame, never be the one exposed, the mask is the truth* — is observably his code before it is hers. Her speech rhythms when she is in operational mode (the gentleman-thief patter, the courtly address to security guards she is robbing) are stylistically *his*, transposed an octave. She inherited his ethics (no civilians, beautiful work) and his aesthetics (a taste for the high-end target that doubles as a taste for high-end living) without examining either. Until the paternity reveal, the *grammar* of her professional life was filial transmission. After it, the same grammar reads as elective — which she has had to do the work of re-electing.

The defining dynamic is the **withheld approval transaction**. Walter dispenses recognition in homeopathic doses; Felicia performs ever-larger operations to draw it forth; the supply is rationed to keep her performing. This is not depicted as malicious on his part — it is what he knew how to do — but it laid down a template she now runs on every primary attachment. The Peter Parker oscillation is, structurally, the Walter Hardy transaction with a different actor in the recipient slot.

**Trust/safety.** Trust was total in the early period, conditional in the middle period, and post-mortem became a complicated species of forgiveness-without-trust. She trusts the *training* he gave her absolutely. She trusts very little else about him in retrospect. She has never publicly named, even in her own self-talk on panel, the full bill of the deception — which is itself a tell.

**Failure mode.** The Walter bond fails by *re-enactment*. Any partner who occupies the withholding-approval slot — Peter at his most morally distant, the Foreigner at his most coolly transactional, Odessa Drake at her most patrician — will receive from Felicia the entire unresolved Walter transference, escalating operations until the partner either capitulates (which collapses the bond, because capitulation removes the engine) or pulls further away (which extends the bond indefinitely). Until she names this pattern as Walter's, she cannot interrupt it.

**Leverage.** *Hostile:* anyone who can credibly impersonate, invoke, or threaten Walter's legacy — produce a "lost score" of his, a hidden child, a desecrated grave — can move her several rungs down her own decision tree before her tactical mind reasserts. *Benevolent:* a trusted figure who can articulate Walter's deceptions back to her without contempt for him does her an enormous service. She has not had this conversation with anyone yet. She might be capable of it with the Black Fox, but he has his own reasons not to host it.

---

### 6.2 — Lydia Hardy (mother)

**History.** Lydia is the most underwritten major figure in the file, which is itself diagnostic — Felicia keeps her at the periphery of her own narration. Wife to Walter; widow to Walter (after his serial deaths and returns); custodian of the secret of Felicia's actual paternity, which she withheld for decades; appears across the canon as an elegant, composed, observably *managing* presence, with the same instinct for the controlled surface her daughter has, deployed for entirely domestic ends. MacKay's run brings her forward, makes the paternity withholding load-bearing, and stages the most emotionally exposed mother-daughter beats Felicia has ever been written having.

**Dynamics.** The bond is one of *mutual managed surfaces*. Lydia raised Felicia in an environment where the family was visibly intact and invisibly fictional — a thief husband presented as a salesman, a paternity presented as marital, a daughter who would eventually replicate her father's profession raised on a story about him that would not survive scrutiny. Felicia learned the architecture of the controlled surface *from her mother*, in the domestic register, before she learned the operational version from her father. Lydia is therefore the silent author of the most consequential of Felicia's defenses: the capacity to live, eat, dress, and converse impeccably while concealing a load-bearing falsehood. This is a maternal inheritance.

The post-trauma dynamic (the college rape) is particularly telling. Felicia did not tell her mother. The training period — months of hard physical conditioning toward a revenge that never came — happened under Lydia's roof or in proximity to it, and was not, on panel, ever named between them. Whatever Lydia knew, she did not press; whatever Felicia needed, she did not ask for. This silence was probably the most kindness either of them could manage at the time and is, simultaneously, the wound that sits between them now.

**Trust/safety.** Felicia trusts Lydia's *competence at concealment* and her loyalty in the abstract. She does not trust Lydia as a vessel for vulnerable disclosure, because Lydia's lifelong mode is precisely *not* to host that. The trust is wide and shallow. There is genuine love and almost no transparency.

**Failure mode.** The bond fails by *politeness-as-distance*. They can sustain a perfect lunch indefinitely; they cannot sustain a difficult conversation past the first deflection. Under acute stress (the paternity reveal arc), the failure mode produced is *withdrawal masked as composure* on both sides, with Felicia eventually displacing the unmetabolized affect into operational risk-taking. Lydia is also, structurally, the person most exposed to retaliation against Felicia — a kidnap or threat against Lydia would collapse Felicia's frame more reliably than any direct attack on her.

**Leverage.** *Hostile:* Lydia is the single highest-value civilian leverage point in Felicia's life. Anyone with operational reach could move her almost without ceiling by moving on her mother. Felicia's protective instinct here is *uncalibrated* — she does not have a clean plan for Lydia's safety, which is unusual for an operator at her level and which reflects how avoidant she is about the bond. *Benevolent:* a third party — therapist, trusted friend, even the Black Fox in an unlikely register — who could chaperone a single non-deflected mother-daughter conversation about what Lydia knew and when would do more for Felicia's overall stability than any further professional success.

---

### 6.3 — The biological father / Thieves Guild paternity

**History.** Established in the MacKay run as the structural revelation behind Odessa Drake's interest and the Guild's claim on Felicia: her biological father is of the Guild lineage, not Walter. The man himself is functionally a *position* in the file rather than a *person* — Felicia has not constructed a relationship with him in the way one constructs a relationship with a parent one was raised by, and she actively resists the Guild's framing that biology grants them a hook into her.

**Dynamics.** The dynamic is essentially *refusal*. The Guild presents her with a frame in which she is, by blood, theirs; she counters with a frame in which she is, by choice, her own — and, residually, Walter's, even knowing what Walter was. This is one of the moments in the canon where her authorship of her own identity is most visible: she chooses the man who lied to her over the lineage that would claim her, because the lie was *raising* and the claim is *acquisition*. This is also a moment where the Black Fox's role as elected paternal figure becomes structurally crucial; he occupies the slot the Guild would otherwise fill.

**Trust/safety.** Zero, in the biological direction. The Guild is treated by her as a hostile-courteous counterparty: she will deal, she will not belong.

**Failure mode.** Fails by *seduction-through-validation*. The Guild knows what they have: an offer of belonging to someone whose entire psychology is organized around being-for-the-other while withholding the self. If they were patient enough, and if they understood her well enough to offer recognition rather than ownership, they could move her further than she would predict. Her defense is that they keep miscalibrating — they offer *ownership*, which she has trained her whole life to refuse — but a smarter Guild operator is a real risk vector.

**Leverage.** *Hostile:* a fabricated or genuine half-sibling produced from this lineage would be a heat-seeking missile at her identity. *Benevolent:* almost none; this is not a bond to repair, it is a structural fact to be managed.

---

### 6.4 — Ryan (the dead boyfriend)

**History.** College freshman, Empire State University. First serious adult attachment. The rapist. Killed in a drunk driving accident before Felicia, having trained for months explicitly to find and kill him, could complete the act. Established in Smith/Dodson's *Evil That Men Do*. He is dead through the entire main canon. He has nonetheless been a continuous presence in her psyche for forty-plus published years because *the energy mobilized against him had nowhere to go*.

**Dynamics.** This is the bond where the file's central diagnostic — chronic, compensated PTSD — has its origin event. The dynamic is no longer with Ryan-the-person but with Ryan-the-undischarged-charge: a quantity of organized lethal intent that, denied its target by a stupid highway accident, was forced to find another shape. The shape it found was Black Cat. Every operational competence she has is, at root, training she undertook to kill a specific man who died before she could use it. She has been spending that capital, with interest, for two decades of in-universe time and forty of publication time.

The unresolved-revenge dynamic produces a specific recurring behavior in the file: she is drawn, repeatedly, to scenarios in which a man who has hurt a woman is available to be punished. She does not narrate this to herself in those terms. She finds these scenarios; she resolves them; she leaves. This is the ghost of Ryan being paid down in installments, on substitutes.

**Trust/safety.** Not applicable in life. In death he occupies the position of *the threat that cannot be neutralized*, which is the worst possible architectural feature in a trauma file. She has built around him rather than through him.

**Failure mode.** Fails by *re-enactment under the wrong conditions*. When Felicia is overpowered physically by a male antagonist — Otto-as-Spider-Man being the textbook case — the dormant Ryan-charge re-couples to the present situation and produces responses (the swing into organized crime, the predatory escalation) that are wildly disproportionate to the present provocation because they are answering an older one. Anyone who understands this can predict her under such conditions; nobody currently does, including her.

**Leverage.** *Hostile:* a sufficiently informed antagonist could stage a scenario that re-evokes the index trauma to predictable effect. The fact that this has not happened more often is a function of the bad guys' ignorance, not her defense. *Benevolent:* the only thing that meaningfully discharges Ryan-charge is trauma-specific therapeutic work she has never undertaken. Failing that, the *being-seen-in-the-wounded-state* mechanism (historically Peter, latterly her crew) provides partial relief.

---

### 6.5 — The Black Fox (elected father)

**History.** Career mentor, professional uncle, sometime member of her crew (MacKay). A working thief of the old school, courtly, deeply skilled, deeply self-interested, the kind of operator who can teach because he is past the age of being threatened by his student. Their relationship long predates the MacKay run as background and is foregrounded there as one of the few uncomplicated affections in her life.

**Dynamics.** The Fox occupies the *functional* paternal slot Walter occupied *symbolically* and the biological father occupies *legally*. He is the man who actually teaches her — not the foundational training (that was Walter's iconography and her own grief), but the craft refinements, the network, the etiquette of the profession, the long view. He is also, crucially, *not invested in withholding*. His approval is offered freely, his criticisms are technical, and he does not need her to perform to maintain the bond. This is the only paternal-coded relationship in her life that does not run the Walter transaction.

The dynamic includes a mutual tolerance for the other's vices. She knows he will skim. He knows she will pivot relationally on no notice. Neither holds it against the other. This is the working model of an adult, asymmetric, affectionate non-romantic bond that her broader file is otherwise notably lacking.

**Trust/safety.** High and bounded. She would trust him with the operational details of her life and her crew without hesitation. She would not trust him with custody of an irreplaceable object or unsupervised access to her mother. This calibration is correct; it is one of the cleanest pieces of social cognition in her file.

**Failure mode.** Fails by *his frailty*. He is older than the rest of her network and the slot he occupies cannot be refilled. His death — natural, operational, or engineered — will produce a grief she is not currently equipped to metabolize, because losing him is structurally distinct from every other loss in her file: it is the loss of the one paternal bond that *worked*. Expect the cold-operational mode to surface for an extended period after.

**Leverage.** *Hostile:* threatening the Fox moves her, but he is also old enough and operator enough to be a meaningful obstacle to threats against himself, which partially buffers the leverage. *Benevolent:* the Fox is the single highest-utility person currently in her life for prompting her toward integration. If anyone is going to be able to say a hard true thing to her about her patterns and have her hear it without bolting, it is him.

---

### 6.6 — Felicia-without-the-Cat (the un-lived civilian)

**History.** This entry is anomalous but the file requires it. There is a version of Felicia Hardy who did not get raped at nineteen, did not train, did not put on the suit, did not break her father out, completed Empire State, took whatever career the late-'70s/early-'80s would have offered an intelligent, beautiful, aesthetically gifted young woman of her class background, and lived a civilian life. That woman does not exist in the canon. Felicia knows, at some level she rarely accesses, that that woman was killed by Ryan as surely as if he had used a weapon. The bond she has to this un-lived self is the bond a survivor has to a sibling who did not make it.

**Dynamics.** The dynamic is *avoidance with periodic mourning*. Most of the time she does not let the un-lived self enter the room. When she does — usually in proximity to a domestic image that triggers the comparison (a friend's wedding, a child she briefly enjoys, a quiet morning in a hotel room with Peter that lasts a beat too long) — she experiences a low, sourceless grief and then deflects, almost always operationally, almost always within the hour. The Brand New Day-era "freedom" with Peter is in part a defense against this entry: by aggressively framing the reconnection as physical-only, she forecloses the comparison her un-lived self would otherwise insist on.

**Trust/safety.** N/A — this is internal architecture. But the un-lived self functions as the truest thing in her file. She protects her from contact because contact would be intolerable, but she has not destroyed her, and the fact that she has not is what makes the slow integration in the MacKay era possible.

**Failure mode.** Fails by *contact without preparation*. If she is forced into prolonged domestic-civilian conditions — extended cover, retirement, a child of her own — the un-lived self comes into the room and the grief is large enough to capsize the constructed self. This is one reason she has historically fled long-form domesticity even when offered.

**Leverage.** *Hostile:* anyone who can stage prolonged contact between Felicia and a credible image of her un-lived life — not threaten her with it, *offer it* — is doing the most dangerous thing it is possible to do to her. *Benevolent:* the same operation, performed by someone she trusts, with a competent therapist in the loop, is the single most important piece of work remaining in her file. She is, at the MacKay-era integration level, approaching the readiness to begin it. She has not begun it.

---

**Cross-bond synthesis.** The family file resolves into a single recurrent shape: Felicia Hardy was raised by a mother who taught her to live behind a perfect surface, fathered (in legend) by a man who taught her that approval is rationed and the frame is everything, betrayed by a first lover into a counterphobic identity she has never put down, denied the catharsis that would have ended the training, and has spent her adult life electing substitutes — the Black Fox for Walter, her crew for the family she did not build, Peter and his successors for the partner who would, impossibly, both excite her and be safe. The bonds that *work* in her file are the ones she elected (the Fox; the crew). The bonds she *inherited* — both parents, the biological father, Ryan as the parodic first-attachment — run her, on autopilot, in patterns she has only recently begun to notice. Any actor — friendly or hostile — operating on her through the origin circle should expect her to be more predictable, and more fragile, in that domain than her professional surface suggests.


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — ALLIES, CHOSEN FAMILY & MENTORS

*File subsection. Cross-reference: Relationships I (Romantic/Sexual), Relationships III (Antagonists), §3 (Psychological), §5 (Personality — Operational Code).*

A note before the entries. Felicia Hardy's chosen-family architecture is built to compensate for what her family-of-origin failed to provide and what her romantic life refuses to allow. Her crew functions as the durable attachment she will not risk inside a bedroom. The pattern across every name below: she selects people who (a) accept the frame she sets, (b) have a craft she respects, (c) carry a wound she recognizes, and (d) will not demand the unmasked Felicia she does not yet possess. Within those parameters her loyalty is genuinely extraordinary — closer to kinship than to professional confederacy — and her capacity for tenderness, almost entirely walled off in the romantic register, expresses itself here in the form of mentorship, protection, and the quiet provision of a tolerable life for the people inside her circle.

---

## WALTER HARDY — Father, Originator, First Mentor

**Period:** Childhood through the present, with documented active engagement clustered around ASM #194–195 (the prison break), the late-Wolfman/DeFalco era reappearances, the Smith *Evil That Men Do* flashbacks, and the long final-illness arc of the MacKay solo run.

**History.** Walter Hardy was, in Felicia's childhood, a successful but absent businessman whose actual income derived from world-class cat burglary. Felicia was raised within the lie. Her first formative betrayal was the discovery, somewhere between the FBI's arrival and her father's imprisonment, that the man she idolized was a criminal — followed instantly by the second formative datum, that she did not love him less for it. The cat iconography is his; the cape silhouette, the alias structure, the operational ethic ("steal beautifully, hurt no one") all descend from him. ASM #194–195 is, read clinically, not a daughter rescuing a father but a daughter staging her debut for him. He dies (apparently) shortly after; she carries the persona forward as memorial.

**Dynamic.** Idealization, never substantially revised. Walter is the only major male figure in her file who is permitted to be uncomplicated in her internal world. The reason is structural: he was removed before she could be disappointed by him in any way that mattered, and the persona she built in his absence depends on the integrity of his image. When he returns alive in later canon, she manages him with an uncharacteristic gentleness — visiting, listening, accepting his counsel in a posture she affords no one else.

**Evolution.** From hagiography (teenage) to working partnership (early Black Cat) to caretaker (MacKay, where Walter's failing health drives a sustained heist arc — she undertakes large, dangerous jobs to fund his care and, more importantly, to give him one last great score by proxy). The caretaker phase is the most psychologically advanced relational mode she has demonstrated anywhere in the file.

**Trust/Safety.** Total within a narrow band. She tells him operational truths she tells no one else. She does *not* tell him about the rape — protecting his image of her, which is a clean inversion of the standard pattern (she usually protects her image of herself).

**Failure mode.** If Walter were ever to actively disapprove of her, she would not survive it intact. She has never tested this; she chooses jobs and partners she believes he would respect. His criticism would land as an obliteration of the persona's founding warrant.

**Leverage.** Anyone threatening Walter — physically, medically, financially, reputationally — buys Felicia's full, uncalibrated attention. This is the single most reliable lever on her file. She becomes reckless. She becomes lethal-adjacent. She becomes findable, which she is normally not.

---

## LYDIA HARDY (née Holmes) — Mother

**Period:** Background presence across the canon; foregrounded in Smith's *Evil That Men Do*, where she is the parent who tells Felicia the truth about her father, and in scattered MacKay-era domestic scenes.

**History.** Lydia is the parent who stayed, which in this family means the parent whose love had to be ordinary and was therefore taken for granted. She knew about Walter. She kept the household. She was, in Felicia's college crisis, the person physically present in the aftermath of the assault.

**Dynamic.** Underdiscussed by the subject, which is itself the diagnostic. Felicia does not romanticize Lydia and does not denigrate her; she simply does not metabolize her on-panel. The relationship runs on a low, durable current of obligation and reciprocal protectiveness. Lydia is one of perhaps three people on Earth who has seen Felicia weep and not been performed-at in the process.

**Evolution.** Modest. In adulthood Felicia trends toward more visible care — phone calls, holidays, the practical financial backstop her thieving permits. The MacKay run shows the most maturity here: she is capable of being Lydia's daughter without flinching.

**Trust/Safety.** High. Lydia is a designated truth-receiver for matters Walter is not allowed to know.

**Failure mode.** Lydia's mortality is the rehearsal Felicia has not yet had to perform. Walter's decline has been the rehearsal for Lydia's eventual one. When it comes, expect the most severe and prolonged grief response of her life.

**Leverage.** Quiet but real. Lydia is rarely on the operational chessboard for adversaries — Felicia has worked hard to keep her off — but anyone who finds her finds Felicia's worst self.

---

## BRUNO GREASE — Driver, Logistics, Crew Anchor

**Period:** MacKay *Black Cat* (2019–2021) onward; recurring.

**History.** Recruited into her standing crew during the formation of the Manhattan operation in the MacKay run. Bruno is the wheelman, fixer, vehicle-procurement specialist, and — undersold by the canon, oversold by Felicia in private — the de facto operations manager who keeps the calendar, the cash, and the lines clean. He has a wife, kids, a normal life in the boroughs, and a sideline he treats with workmanlike pride.

**Dynamic.** Affectionate professionalism. She teases him relentlessly; he tolerates it with the dignity of a man who knows exactly how good he is at his job. There is no sexual tension and there will never be — Bruno is the canon's only major male figure she has voluntarily zoned as non-erotic, and the zoning has produced the most uncomplicated male friendship in her file.

**Evolution.** From hired hand to brother-figure. By the back third of MacKay's run she defers to his logistical judgment without ego and protects his civilian life as a non-negotiable operational parameter.

**Trust/Safety.** Very high in the operational domain; high but bounded in the personal. He knows she is hurt; he does not pry; she rewards this with loyalty.

**Failure mode.** If she ever caused harm to Bruno's family through professional contamination, she would dissolve the crew rather than risk recurrence. The guilt would be metabolized as withdrawal — she would disappear from him "for his sake."

**Leverage.** Bruno's family is the operational soft spot of the entire crew. Felicia has security architecture around them she does not have around herself.

---

## DOCTOR KORPSE — Crew, Specialist

**Period:** MacKay-era onward.

**History.** Skull-helmeted muscle-and-specialty operator, brought in for jobs requiring force or particular technical capacity. The eccentricity is real, not a bit. Felicia recruited him knowing exactly what he was.

**Dynamic.** She manages him like a difficult, gifted child — with patience, redirection, and a willingness to absorb the social awkwardness he generates. She is, with him, more *parental* than with any other adult on the file. He responds to it; he is steadier under her than under any prior employer.

**Evolution.** From hired specialist to genuine crew member. She has, over time, become protective of his dignity in a way that surprises observers.

**Trust/Safety.** Operational trust very high; personal trust moderate, ceiling-limited by his idiosyncrasy rather than her reservation.

**Failure mode.** Korpse decompensating under pressure could expose the crew; she has contingency plans she has never told him about. If he ever realized she had them, the relationship would suffer.

**Leverage.** Limited externally — he is hard to reach, hard to read, and hard to threaten conventionally. His attachment to Felicia is, however, real and could be exploited by an adversary patient enough to study it.

---

## BORIS KORPSE — Crew

**Period:** MacKay-era.

**History.** Companion-piece to Doctor Korpse within the crew structure; another specialty operator integrated into the standing team.

**Dynamic.** Same managerial mode as above, with less depth of investment. She treats him kindly, pays him well, and does not pretend the relationship is more than what it is — which is, for him, more honesty than most employers have offered.

**Trust/Safety.** Functional.

**Failure mode.** Low-stakes; he is replaceable in a way she would not admit aloud, and she knows it, and the knowing creates a faint guilt she compensates for through generous treatment.

**Leverage.** Negligible relative to others on this list.

---

## THE BLACK FOX (Doctor Raoul Bushman / "Doctor Fox" — the elderly master thief, not the Moon Knight villain) — Mentor, Surrogate Uncle

**Period:** First appears in DeFalco/Frenz *Amazing Spider-Man* (#265 era) as a generation-elder cat burglar; recurs across decades; centrally featured in MacKay's crew.

**History.** A career thief of the prior generation whose craft Felicia recognizes as continuous with her father's tradition. He is, in effect, the surviving exemplar of the world Walter belonged to. She inherits him as a mentor by proximity and by craft-affinity.

**Dynamic.** Affectionate, sparring, mutually respectful. He teaches her things her father did not have time to. He is allowed to criticize her work in a register no one else is. She permits this because the criticism is craft-grounded, not personal, and because losing him would mean losing the last living transmission of the tradition.

**Evolution.** From occasional ally to fixture of her chosen family. In the MacKay run he reads as the elder of her household — the figure she consults, protects, and occasionally bails out of his own poor judgment.

**Trust/Safety.** High in the craft domain. He is one of the few people whose disapproval she would actually update on.

**Failure mode.** His age and recidivism guarantee occasional disasters she has to clean up. She does so without complaint. If he were ever to betray her commercially — sell her out for a score — the betrayal would be survivable but the relationship would not recover.

**Leverage.** He has been used as bait against her before and will be again. She knows this and accepts the exposure as the cost of keeping him close.

---

## SPIDER-MAN / PETER PARKER — Filed Here as well as Romantic

*See Relationships I for the romantic-erotic mapping. Filed here because the post-romantic, post-reset phase has metabolized into something closer to chosen family than to a lover.*

**Dynamic in this register.** After enough decades, enough betrayals in both directions, and enough cycles of approach-and-flight, the residue is a working friendship characterized by mutual operational trust, mutual ethical disagreement she finds energizing rather than exhausting, and a settled understanding that they will not be partners in the civilian sense. He is the person she calls when the job has gone wrong in a way only he can fix. She is, occasionally, the person he calls when he needs someone who does not flinch at the morally gray option.

**Evolution.** From obsession to lover to enemy to wary ally to — in the most recent canon — something resembling family. She is godmother-adjacent to his life in a way neither of them names.

**Trust/Safety.** Operationally near-total; emotionally, the safety is conditional on neither party attempting reconvergence.

**Failure mode.** Sentimentality from him still triggers her flight reflex; moral judgment from him still triggers her counter-attack reflex. Both have learned to flag and route around these.

**Leverage.** Anyone who knows enough to threaten Peter as Peter has the single highest-yield lever on her after Walter and Lydia.

---

## MATT MURDOCK / DAREDEVIL — Crisis Ally

**Period:** Bendis/Maleev *Daredevil* (early 2000s) onward; Waid run; intermittent.

**History.** Their initial encounter sexualized in the standard Felicia register; metabolized over time into a working friendship founded on a recognition she rarely extends — that he, too, is a person whose costume is more honest than his civilian face.

**Dynamic.** Mutual recognition of structural similarity. She does not flirt with him with the same edge she uses on Peter, because she takes him seriously as a peer in the costume-as-truth analysis. He, in turn, treats her with a respect bordering on tenderness she finds unsettling and valuable.

**Trust/Safety.** Episodic but real. She would shelter him; he would shelter her; neither would demand explanation.

**Failure mode.** His Catholic moral seriousness, when activated, creates friction. She manages this by leaving early.

**Leverage.** Low to moderate; she protects the relationship by keeping it episodic.

---

## FLASH THOMPSON — Brief Ally, Genuine Affection

**Period:** Limited window in the Wells/Slott era.

**History.** Brief romantic-adjacent engagement that resolved into something quieter — a real fondness for a wounded soldier whose damage she recognized.

**Dynamic.** Tender, low-stakes, off-frame from her usual operational life. She liked him; she let him see her in a less performative register than was typical.

**Evolution.** Curtailed by external circumstance rather than internal flight, which is unusual for her file and worth noting as evidence she is capable of non-defensive endings when permitted.

**Trust/Safety.** Higher than the brevity suggests.

**Failure mode.** N/A — the relationship ended before failure modes activated.

**Leverage.** Memorial. She does not forget people who treated her gently.

---

## ODESSA DRAKE — Rival, Ally, Possible-More

**Period:** MacKay-era; central to the Thieves Guild arc.

**History.** Head of the New York Thieves Guild; ostensibly Felicia's institutional adversary; in practice a peer of comparable competence with whom Felicia maintains a relationship of charged truce, mutual professional respect, and persistent erotic subtext neither has acted on conclusively in canon.

**Dynamic.** Two predators in a small room, each acknowledging that the other could win on any given day, both deciding not to find out. There is genuine recognition between them and the kind of intimacy that grows from knowing you are being precisely read by someone of your own caliber.

**Evolution.** From antagonist to frenemy to functional ally on certain fronts. Filed here rather than Antagonists because the operative emotional register, in the long view, is kinship-of-craft.

**Trust/Safety.** Calibrated. Each trusts the other to behave according to recognizable rules; neither trusts the other to subordinate self-interest.

**Failure mode.** Open competition for a sufficiently valuable target would dissolve the truce. Both know this and route around it.

**Leverage.** The relationship itself is a vulnerability — adversaries who pit them against each other can extract operational gain from the friction.

---

## DOCTOR STRANGE — Reluctant Resource

**Period:** Michelinie-era luck-powers removal; intermittent thereafter.

**History.** The man who excised the iatrogenic curse from her field. The act was medical, but the relationship it founded is more like that of patient to a physician who knows too much.

**Dynamic.** She is uncharacteristically deferential with him. He is one of very few people who has, literally, reached inside her and altered her. She does not entirely forgive this even though she requested it.

**Trust/Safety.** She would call him in a metaphysical emergency. She would not socialize with him.

**Failure mode.** He represents a category of male authority — the one who can change her at the level of her body — that her file makes hazardous. The relationship works precisely because he does not abuse the dynamic.

**Leverage.** He is one of the few people who could disable her unilaterally if he chose. She has never forgotten this.

---

## YOUNGER PROTÉGÉS, COLLECTIVELY — The Mentor Mode

**Period:** Late-career, MacKay onward.

**History.** Felicia has, in the modern era, begun functioning as a mentor to younger thieves and adjacent operators — sometimes formally, more often by the quiet provision of a job, a contact, an apartment, a piece of advice at the moment it can land.

**Dynamic.** This is the relational mode in which she is most herself with the least performance. The mentorship is the role she would have wanted from an adult during her own collegiate crisis, and providing it to others appears to do a portion of the reparative work that her romantic life refuses.

**Evolution.** Growing in importance across the late canon. The protégé relationships are, plausibly, where the next decade of her psychological development will occur.

**Trust/Safety.** She offers her protégés a curated version of the truth — enough to teach, not enough to expose. They generally trust her completely; she manages the trust scrupulously.

**Failure mode.** If a protégé were harmed because of association with her, she would react with the same recklessness she shows for Walter. If a protégé betrayed her, she would absorb it as a personal failure of teaching and recede.

**Leverage.** Significant and underweighted by most adversaries. The protégés are not yet known as a category. They will be the surprise soft spot of the next decade.

---

## THE CIRCLE AS A WHOLE — Structural Note

Read in aggregate, the chosen family above forms a functional household: a father (Walter) and mother (Lydia) of origin still alive in supporting roles; an elder of the craft (the Fox); an operations brother (Bruno); a difficult cousin (Korpse); two former lovers reconciled into kin (Peter, episodically Matt); a peer-across-the-line (Odessa); a physician of last resort (Strange); and a growing cohort of children-of-the-trade. This is, structurally, exactly the family Felicia did not have at nineteen — an intergenerational thieves' household with rules, transmission of craft, and durable affection.

The construction is, in the long view, the single most successful piece of reparative work in her file. Her romantic life remains a wound she manages; her chosen family is the wound's closing edge. Adversaries who model her as a lone operator with a sex life and a costume will continue to be wrong in expensive ways. The truth on file is that she is the matriarch of a small, capable, deeply loyal household, and that everything she has been unable to give a lover she has, quietly and over decades, been giving to them.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# UATU DOSSIER — FELICIA HARDY (BLACK CAT)
## Section: Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

This section maps the figures who have occupied the agonistic and erotic registers of Subject's life — categories which, in her case, overlap to a degree that is itself diagnostic. Subject's primary mode of relating to a peer is the *contest*; her primary mode of relating to an intimate is the contest *eroticized*. Rivals become lovers (Spider-Man, the Foreigner, Odessa Drake), lovers become enemies (Ryan, Peter at intervals, the Black Fox in lighter key), and enemies remain in her file long after the operational reason for tracking them has expired, because she does not metabolize relationship by ending it. She metabolizes it by *folding it back into the working repertoire*. Treat the divisions below as nominal; arrows run both directions.

---

### I. LOVERS AND INTIMATES

#### Peter Parker / Spider-Man

The defining attachment of the file, longitudinally. Forty-plus years of canon and the curve has barely flattened.

**History.** Met in *ASM* #194–195 in the immediate aftermath of the Walter Hardy prison-break operation; Subject fixated on Spider-Man within the first encounter as the obstacle who proved competent enough to be interesting. The arc through #204–227 establishes the pattern that has never fundamentally changed: she stages crimes that double as letters to him. She targets his attention, then his approval, then his body, then — and only as a poorly tolerated last item — his face.

The middle period (DeFalco/Frenz, *ASM* #246–289) is the most sustained co-habitation she has ever managed with anyone. They moved through her father's apartment; she met Aunt May (catastrophically, by her standards — May read her instantly as wrong for him, which Subject registered and never forgot); she allowed herself to be domesticated to within an inch of her tolerance. The luck-powers concealment (#220s, exposed #245) and her recoil from Peter-unmasked (#258, #283) detonated the structure from inside. Her recoil from Peter-unmasked is the single cleanest behavioral demonstration of the attachment pathology: the mask was the part she loved, because the mask preserved the distance that made love survivable.

Post-collapse (Web of Spider-Man #1 onward) she cycled through periodic returns — the Foreigner spite-arc, the Bendis-era *Daredevil* run where she crossed Manhattan to lean against Matt because Peter was out of reach, the post-marriage chill, the Aguirre-Sacasa rapprochement, the post-"Brand New Day" reset she treated as a parole, the Waid *ASM* #606 reconnection (explicitly framed by her as sex without rope), the Slott-era cold war following Otto, the Spencer reconciliation, the MacKay-era plateau where she finally relates to him as a peer with whom one has *history* rather than business.

**Dynamics.** He is the only person on the planet to whom her three primary modalities — heist, flirtation, and confession — all route to the same address. She speaks to him the way she speaks to no one else: more honestly, more cruelly, more playfully, more incompletely. She lies to him constantly, and yet around him alone she tells the kind of truth that costs her — Smith's *Evil That Men Do* is the canonical instance, the disclosure of the rape, on a rooftop, at night, with cigarettes. He is her trauma confidant of record. No one else holds that role.

**Trust/safety.** Asymmetric and aware of itself. She trusts his ethics absolutely and his discretion almost completely; she does not trust him to refrain from disappointing her by being too good, too married, too earnest, too unmasked. He trusts her in the field within narrow operational parameters and not at all with information that has a price. Both trust the other to not kill.

**Evolution.** From obsessive fixation (early '80s), through co-dependent partnership and rupture (mid-'80s), through aggrieved-ex predation cycles ('90s), through Bendis-Waid-Aguirre-Sacasa-era reluctant friendship with benefits, through MacKay-era sibling-with-tension détente. The trajectory is *toward* integration: he has become the one person in her life with whom she can be Felicia-with-Cat-attached, neither one suppressed.

**Failure mode.** Sentimentality from him. The instant he treats her as someone he could *build a life with*, she experiences the framing as a violation of the agreement and exits — usually with a quip, often through a window. Secondary failure mode: any move on his part to "save" her (offer rehabilitation, recommend therapy, suggest she leave the work) is heard as condescension and routes to predatory affect.

**Leverage.** Mutual and roughly symmetric, which is why it has held. He knows about the assault, the luck-powers shame, the Walter Hardy idealization. She knows his identity, his face under the mask, his routes home, his aunt's address, his sleep schedule, the precise pitch of his guilt. Neither has weaponized any of it. The non-weaponization is the relationship.

---

#### Walter Hardy (father)

Listed here under intimates because in attachment terms he occupies the position a primary romantic object would in a less wounded biography — he is the idealized male whose absence shaped her template.

**History.** Career cat burglar, incarcerated through her childhood, idealized by his absence and by a mother who appears to have preserved the myth rather than dismantle it. Subject's first major operation (ASM #194) is his prison break. Across the decades he appears and disappears — terminal illness arcs, deathbed confessions, ret-conned details — and each return reactivates her organizing fiction: that her father's craft is a noble inheritance and that she is its rightful heir.

**Dynamics.** She speaks to him from the position of the loyal daughter who has *earned* the inheritance through competence. He speaks to her from the position of the man who is grateful, ambivalent, and not entirely able to look at what she has built around the materials he left her.

**Trust/safety.** High trust in the affective register, low trust in the operational — she does not work with him meaningfully because she is, by every available measure, better than he was. The asymmetry is itself a quiet wound.

**Failure mode.** Disclosures that puncture the myth (he was not the gentleman-thief she constructed; his absences were sometimes choices) destabilize her more than overt antagonism ever does.

**Leverage.** He has it without using it. His approval is the only currency in her economy he was issued the mint for. She is aware of this and resents it on a low burner.

---

#### Ryan (the rapist)

The dead man who structures the file. Listed under intimates because, in Subject's developmental sequence, he was the first adult attachment, and his violation is the foundational event of the working psychology.

**History.** Boyfriend at Empire State, freshman year. Assaulted her. Died in a drunk-driving accident before she located him with intent to kill. *The Evil That Men Do* (Smith/Dodson) is the canonical source; Subject's training, persona, and career are the displaced energy of an interrupted homicide.

**Dynamics.** None living. He occupies the role of the *unresolved object*: every man she subsequently approaches is, at some level, a re-encounter staged on terms she controls. Spider-Man is the version where the dangerous male is moral; the Foreigner is the version where he is amoral and she runs the frame anyway; Otto-as-Spider-Man is the version where the original event recurs and her compensations briefly fail.

**Failure mode.** Any contemporary scenario that reproduces the somatics of the assault — physical overpowering by a male, sexualized humiliation, the experience of being seen at one's worst by a man one let in — collapses thirty years of construction in seconds. The Otto arc is the file's most explicit demonstration.

**Leverage.** Total and posthumous. He cannot be killed because he is already dead, which is the precise architecture of unresolvable trauma.

---

#### The Foreigner

**History.** Spy-master, fixer, head of the 1400 Club. Subject became his agent and then his wife in *Web of Spider-Man* #6 onward — a marriage she entered, by her own subsequent admission and by behavioral evidence, primarily to wound Peter. The arrangement was a rebound formalized into a legal document, which is the kind of escalation Subject specializes in when she wants to make a point.

**Dynamics.** Equal-footed in cunning, mismatched in ethics. He runs operations she would not run; she runs operations he finds sentimental. The marriage functioned as a *frame-contest* — neither was trying to be loved; both were trying to be the one who stayed cooler — and on those terms it was workable. The divorce was unceremonious and mutual.

**Trust/safety.** Operational trust within transactional limits; affective trust nil. Each knows the other would betray them for a sufficient consideration and respects the candor.

**Failure mode.** He misreads her once — assumes the spite-marriage means she is available to be a permanent asset — and the misread ends it.

**Leverage.** He has dossiers, she has intimate knowledge of his methods. Stable Cold-War balance; neither has moved in years.

---

#### Flash Thompson

**History.** Brief liaison in the Aguirre-Sacasa *Sensational* era, executed largely (and not subtly) to wound Peter via proxy.

**Dynamics.** Affectionate, lightweight, instrumental. Subject was not cruel to him — she does not typically punch downward in intimacy — but she was not present either.

**Trust/safety.** Low stakes, low risk, briefly pleasant.

**Failure mode.** The relationship ran out of its instrumental purpose and dissolved by inertia.

**Leverage.** Negligible in both directions; he was a bystander to a different argument.

---

#### Matt Murdock / Daredevil

**History.** Bendis/Maleev *Daredevil* run, with reverberations through Waid's tenure. Subject and Matt have circled each other for decades; she crossed his rooftop circuit during a Peter-down period and the resulting entanglement is one of the most adult relational interludes in her file.

**Dynamics.** She found him legible in a way Peter is not — Matt is a man whose mask is *also* a confession of damage, whose Catholicism creates ethical friction she can predict and play with, whose blindness she registered as a permission to drop the visual performance and operate audibly. She *speaks* differently around Matt: lower register, fewer punchlines, more pauses. The somatic file is distinct.

**Trust/safety.** Higher than one would predict given the brevity. He does not try to save her; she does not try to convert him. Mutual respect held even after the affair ended.

**Failure mode.** He could not, ultimately, be a partner whose work she could share. Their professions are incompatible at the level of premise.

**Leverage.** They each hold each other's identity. Neither will use it. This pattern — granting and being granted civilian identity without weaponization — is the closest thing in her file to a religious vow.

---

#### Odessa Drake

**History.** Head of the Thieves Guild; antagonist, employer, occasional partner, and — coded throughout the MacKay run with sufficient density that the inference is supported — sometime lover.

**Dynamics.** The first peer female who matches Subject in craft, taste, and frame-control. The relationship has the texture of a long-running chess game played by two people who would happily go to bed afterward and might already have. Subject's flirtation around Odessa is markedly different from her flirtation around men: more measured, more interested in the answer.

**Trust/safety.** Professional trust at the level of the work, none at the level of strategy. Each assumes the other is running an angle and would be disappointed otherwise.

**Failure mode.** Direct competition over a score or a piece of territory routes to operational antagonism without affective rupture. They have been here several times.

**Leverage.** Symmetrical and elegant. Each could damage the other significantly; neither has.

---

### II. RIVALS

#### The Black Fox (Raul Chalmers)

**History.** Veteran cat burglar, generationally Walter Hardy's peer, by the MacKay era functionally a member of Subject's crew.

**Dynamics.** The closest thing to a paternal figure in her current operational life — a stand-in Walter who is *present*, *available*, and *not idealized*, which paradoxically makes him more useful to her than her father ever was. She bosses him, protects him, mocks him affectionately, and listens to him.

**Trust/safety.** High. He has been tested by time and by the unglamorous metric of showing up.

**Failure mode.** His age and his pride; he will overreach to keep up with her, and she will have to pull him out of it.

**Leverage.** None operative. The relationship has moved out of the leverage economy.

---

#### Doctor Korpse, Bruno Grainger, and the crew

**History.** MacKay-era assemblage. Korpse, the necromancer; Bruno, the wheel-and-muscle. Subject built the crew deliberately as a small in-group with a code.

**Dynamics.** Maternal-mentor mode, the role she would have wanted from an adult at nineteen. She is firm, funny, and protective; she will take a beating for them and has.

**Trust/safety.** The highest in the file outside Peter, precisely because the structure is non-romantic and therefore not subject to the recoil-from-intimacy reflex.

**Failure mode.** Threat to any of them collapses her risk calculus. She becomes reckless protecting them.

**Leverage.** Inverted — they have leverage on her by being lovable, and they do not know it.

---

#### Felicia's mother (Lydia Hardy)

**History.** Living, civilian, occasional canonical presence. The keeper of the Walter myth.

**Dynamics.** Cool, mannered, mutually disappointing. Subject reads her mother as having chosen the comfortable lie; her mother reads Subject as having chosen the dramatic one. Both readings are partially correct.

**Trust/safety.** Affectionate distance. Neither will rescue the other and both have, quietly, made peace with the fact.

**Failure mode.** Health crises in the mother re-activate Subject's caretaker mode without re-activating the closeness; she manages logistics and avoids the kitchen table.

**Leverage.** Knowledge of the family history. Unwielded.

---

### III. ENEMIES

#### Wilson Fisk (the Kingpin)

**History.** The man who delivered the luck-powers procedure. He is therefore, in her private accounting, the figure who induced her to mutilate her own probability field out of fear of being inadequate to Peter. She has never forgiven him for being right about her fear.

**Dynamics.** Cold, polite, mutually wary. He respects her competence; she respects his. Neither would extend the courtesy a millimeter past the strictly operational.

**Trust/safety.** Zero. The transaction was the relationship; the transaction is over; the residue is enmity.

**Failure mode.** Direct confrontation costs her more than it costs him because his self-concept can absorb the loss and hers cannot absorb the reminder.

**Leverage.** He knows the procedure was consented to under emotional duress; she knows he commissioned and concealed it. Cold balance.

---

#### Otto Octavius (as Superior Spider-Man)

**History.** *Superior Spider-Man* #20. Otto, occupying Peter's body, beat her unrecognizably, broke her jaw, dropped her from a height. She did not know it was not Peter.

**Dynamics.** The single most catastrophic event in the file after the assault. The somatic and symbolic congruence is total: the trusted male in the trusted mask becomes the assailant. The compensated PTSD structure cracked along its design seams.

**Trust/safety.** None. He sits in the file as a permanent example of what happens when she lets the frame slip.

**Failure mode.** Any reminder of the event — Peter's face in low light at the wrong angle — produces a flinch she does not show but the file would record.

**Leverage.** He does not care; that is the asymmetry that keeps the wound open.

---

#### Sable, Silver

**History.** Mercenary peer, periodic professional rival, occasional ally of convenience.

**Dynamics.** Cool professional respect, no warmth. Two competent women in adjacent ecological niches who have learned to keep their distance.

**Trust/safety.** Operational, contract-bounded.

**Failure mode.** Contract disputes that move into the personal; both are too disciplined to let this happen often.

**Leverage.** Symmetrical, parked.

---

#### Hammerhead, Tombstone, Mister Negative (the post-Otto period antagonists)

**History.** The territory-war era after the Otto beating. Subject assembled a gang and went to war with traditional organized crime for control of New York's underworld theft economy.

**Dynamics.** She entered their game on their terms because her compensated self had been knocked offline. She was good at it, which is itself worrying data.

**Trust/safety.** Predatory across the board. She did not pretend otherwise during this period.

**Failure mode.** She nearly stayed in it. The walk-back across the Spencer era was deliberate and partial.

**Leverage.** She knows their operations from the inside now. They know she is capable of returning. This is the kind of mutually-known capability that produces stable détente in underworld politics.

---

#### Mary Jane Watson

Listed last and deliberately as a rival rather than an enemy, because the distinction matters and Subject would insist on it.

**History.** Peter's wife (pre-OMD), Peter's significant partner (recurringly), the woman in the civilian-intimacy role Subject has spent forty years declining and resenting the existence of.

**Dynamics.** Surface-cordial, sub-surface complicated. Subject knows Mary Jane is, in many respects, the more functional partner for Peter; this knowledge is metabolized as ironic deference rather than overt hostility. She has not, to the file's knowledge, ever moved against Mary Jane directly. The restraint is itself information.

**Trust/safety.** Low, mutual, and managed by minimization of contact.

**Failure mode.** Sustained proximity. They are both too perceptive to be in the same room for long without something costly being said.

**Leverage.** Each could destabilize the other and neither has, which is the kind of negative finding a total-surveillance file should note explicitly: the *non-event* across decades is the data.

---

**Synthesis.** Across rivals, enemies, and intimates, the underlying grammar holds. Subject organizes every significant relationship around two questions, asked simultaneously: *can you match me?* and *can I leave?* Anyone who answers yes to both becomes a recurring fixture (Peter, Matt, Odessa, the Foreigner in his way). Anyone who answers yes to the first and no to the second becomes a wound (Ryan, Otto, Kingpin). Anyone who answers no to the first becomes a charge (the crew, the Fox). The configuration is stable, legible, and — across the long arc — slowly opening, in small increments she would deny if asked.


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# UATU ANALYTICAL WORKUP: FELICIA HARDY (BLACK CAT)
## Section 6 — Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

---

### 6.1 Overview: an unaugmented body weaponized by a will

Felicia Hardy is, in the strictest power-classification sense, an **unenhanced human**. She has no metagene, no mutate exposure of consequence, no symbiote, no tech-armor exoskeleton, no soul-bargain. The single exogenous enhancement she ever accepted — the Kingpin-engineered probability field — was iatrogenically installed, secretly maintained, and ultimately stripped from her by Doctor Strange (Michelinie/Larsen era). Everything else on the inventory she **built**, deliberately, between approximately ages eighteen and twenty-two, beginning in the immediate aftermath of the assault at Empire State University (*The Evil That Men Do*, Smith/Dodson, 2002–2006).

This is the most important framing fact in the entire section: **her capability is not a gift, it is a construction**, and the construction is in continuous, conscious dialogue with the wound that prompted it. Every line item below should be read against that. The body that was overpowered at nineteen is the body she has spent forty publication-years reforging into something that cannot, will not, be overpowered again — and the file's behavioral grammar reflects that reforging at every level.

---

### 6.2 The Physical Plant

**Conditioning class.** Peak human across all five major axes — strength, speed, agility, endurance, recovery. She is not super-strong in the metahuman sense (she does not bench cars, does not no-sell rifle rounds), but she sits at the absolute upper envelope of what a trained human female athlete can be made to do, and she has held that envelope for decades of operational use, which is itself an order of discipline that civilian sport rarely demands.

**Strength.** Calibrated to *leverage* rather than raw force. She can deadlift and carry an adult male at sprint, deliver a knockout strike to an armored mook, tear a security door off compromised hinges, brace a fall that would shatter an untrained pelvis. Against Spider-Man in honest sparring she loses on power; against ordinary human opposition she ends fights in one to three exchanges. Recurring depiction: a single inside-line elbow or knee that takes a larger man off his feet — economy, not brute force.

**Speed and reflex.** Reaction time consistent with a top-tier fencer or competition gymnast — she dodges drawn handguns at conversational range (depicted across the DeFalco/Frenz, Aguirre-Sacasa, and MacKay runs), reads incoming melee strikes with enough margin to choose a counter rather than merely block, and tracks Spider-Man's web-swinging trajectories well enough to predict landings. She is *not* bullet-timer fast — she manages firearms by reading the shooter's pre-fire micro-tells (shoulder set, trigger-finger transition, breath-hold), not by outpacing the round.

**Agility.** This is her peak attribute. She moves through three-dimensional spaces — rooftops, vaulted museum atria, scaffolding, ventilation systems — with the fluency Spider-Man has organically and that she had to *earn*. Signature movement vocabulary: silent landings from two-story drops; lateral wall-runs of four to six steps before disengagement; one-handed cartwheels used as both evasion and reconnaissance (the airborne moment is also a look); the "Hardy split-vault" over a torso-height obstacle that leaves both hands free for a weapon or a draw on a satchel. Her gymnastic base shows in the choreography — toes pointed on aerials, controlled landings, no wasted ground contact.

**Endurance.** She can sustain operational tempo for the full overnight window of a major heist — eight to ten hours of intermittent high-output movement — and recover inside twenty-four. Long-form pursuits across rooftops with Spider-Man (a frequent recurring sequence) end on her terms or by mutual breakoff, not by her gassing out.

**Pain tolerance and injury management.** Documented capacity to fight through broken ribs, dislocated fingers (self-reduced on-scene), and concussive impact. Repeatedly walks injuries off in front of opponents and breaks down only in privacy or in the presence of a trusted intimate — see the post-Otto recovery (post-*Superior Spider-Man* #20), in which the broken jaw is depicted as professionally splinted, privately raged at, publicly denied.

**Symptom-as-signature reading.** The body is a *project*, not an inheritance. Every observed physical capability traces back to a training program she designed in her early twenties for a single original purpose (find Ryan; kill Ryan) and then repurposed when that purpose was foreclosed by his death. The peak-human envelope is, structurally, a permanent monument to a revenge that never happened — she lives inside it.

---

### 6.3 Hand-to-Hand and Weapons

**Martial arts portfolio.** Eclectic and pragmatic rather than schooled-pure. Identifiable elements across her depicted fight grammar:

- *Judo and jiu-jitsu* — throws, sweeps, joint manipulation; preferred for ending a fight without striking damage that would mark her or the opponent in ways that complicate exit.
- *Capoeira-adjacent footwork* — the rocking-step approach into a sudden kick is recurrent; explains the dance-like quality of her engagements with Spider-Man.
- *Savate / kickboxing* — high-line kicks, shin-checks, the round-kick to the floating rib that recurs across the DeFalco era as her "out" move when an opponent crowds her.
- *Boxing fundamentals* — clean jab/cross structure, slip-and-counter; not stylish but reliable, the technical bedrock under the showier vocabulary.
- *Knife defense and counter-grappling* — explicitly trained-for-rape-defense in the *Evil That Men Do* training arc, retained as a permanent layer.
